==PAGE:3==
Abstract
This Thesis explores the application of Field Programmable Gate Array(FPGA) acceler-
ators in the field of Image Processing to maximise the associated performance standards.
The aim of the project is to design a cost-effective minimum latency FPGA Register
Transfer Level(RTL) design to stitch images from Full High Definition Fish-eye camera
lenses.
The project entailed exploration of RAW-image conversion algorithms and Memory
controllers to achieve a low latency and no drop in the frame rate of the video. In
addition, eﬀicient memory controllers are designed to facilitate such high performances.
Resource constraints of the FPGA in use are considered to be the determining factors
for the accelerator design. The resource constraints of peripheral devices are also taken
into account during the design of the project and have been highlighted.

CHAPTER 1
INTRODUCTION
1.1
Overview
It is commonplace for electronic modules to make use of multiple cameras for image
processing. However, the larger resolution output from cameras available commercially
result in large operating frequencies, high memory usage with high data rates. Although,
commercially available image processors may be capable of processing multiple camera
inputs, however, the number of inputs are limited to the resources on board the chip
and its cost. Most common applications, make use of general purpose computers with
multi-core processors to achieve the required high-speed computations.
Although, it is widely noted that most Image Processing algorithms to convert RAW
image data coming from the camera sensors are highly parallel and repetitive, commer-
cial image processors allocate considerable amount of on-board resources to achieve this.
The current industry standard for most commercially available cameras utilize the MIPI
CSI protocol to transfer data between the camera sensors and the processor. The result-
ing high data rate and bandwidth places major constraints on the internal memory of
systems. The idea presented typically deals with such problems with the use of Field
Programmable Gate Arrays(FPGAs) combined with High Speed DDR AXI Memories.
The proposed architecture, in the coming sections, aims to achieve of handling any num-
ber of MIPI interface camera inputs to produce large panoramic images consisting of the
individual images tiled together. The corner stone of the design, is the high parallelism
obtained from FPGAs and high speed memory accesses to handle the incoming pixel
data.
With increasing frame rate requirements for Machine Learning and Artificial Intelli-
gence purposes, limitations arise from the power and resource requirements. Hence, a
resource eﬀicient FPGA solution is proposed to increase speed, reduce power and cost
of the design. In such a case, complex processing are suggested to occur on a dedicated
processor or on a server. The proposed architecture is centered around the popular High
Speed AXI Interface and Embedded Random Access Memories(RAMs).
==PAGE:14==
2
1 INTRODUCTION
1.2
Problem Outline
The initial idea of the project was to combine two video streams from fish-eye lenses
after barrel distortion correction. However, using an FPGA for such correction purposes
would prove to be an impractical way for the following reasons.
Firstly, the parameters determining the distortion produced by fish-eye lenses depend
on the alignment of the lens with the image sensor which would vary between lenses due
to practical diﬀiculties in achieve perfect mounting.
Secondly, these varying parameters would inevitably lead to different correction logic
for every lens. Although, there would be similarities in the logic there would be inherent
differences in the mappings determining the positions of the corrected pixels.
Keeping the above factors in mind, a more pragmatic problem of processing and tiling
any number of MIPI Camera Inputs to produce a larger panoramic image is proposed. It
is noteworthy that most image processing chips used in commercial electronic products
can utmost handle 3 MIPI Lanes while operating with processor frequencies in the range
of GigaHertz and consuming a large amount of power. An FPGA would be a more
practical choice for handling multiple camera inputs while reducing power consumption
and frequency of operation. However, any further image processing would have to be
done on a dedicated processor with the FPGA acting as an accelerator chip for handling
and combining the mutliple video streams.
==PAGE:15==
1.3 Hardware Selection
3
1.3
Hardware Selection
1.3.1
FPGA Development Board
The core design of the thesis deals with multiple video streams, a capable commercial
FPGA development kit with high speed DDR memory and camera interfaces was the
main constraint.
The FPGA Development kit chosen for the project was the Eﬀinix T120F576 De-
velopment Kit with up to 4 MIPI interfaces.
A noteworthy observation is the vast
unavailability of FPGA boards capable of handling multiple MIPI video signals and the
associated bandwidth of 60 frames per second, an FPGA board capable of receiving
2-lane MIPI signals is chosen. Additionally, due to the global shortage at the time of
choosing an FPGA(in September 2021), the Eﬀinix T120F576 Development Kit being
easily available was chosen. Although, there were other boards in the Eﬀinix Trion Series
of FPGAs, the board with maximum specifications such as DDR Memories, Logic Ele-
ments and Embedded Memories was selected to handle multiple incoming video streams.
The Development kit has a hardwired MIPI D-PHY network, that is interfaced di-
rectly to the FPGA core using the provided GUI. The connections to the interfaces
are synthesised using the generated constraints file.
The incoming image is stored in the provided Double Data Rate Static DRAM(DDR3)
Memory using the provided AXI interfaces connected to the FPGA core. The provided
AXI Interfaces to the memory use two sets of interface signals(each capable of both
Read and Write Operations), which are then used for Read and Write operations simul-
taneously. The stored image is then displayed on a monitor using the on board HDMI
Transmitter.
As a result of the HDMI Transmitter capable of transmitting a maximum resolution of
1920x1080, the incoming image is shortened from both cameras to produce a panoramic
1920x1080 resolution.
1.3.2
Camera Sensor Module
The sensor chosen for the project is the Raspberry Pi V2 NoIR Camera with a Sony
IMX 219 Sensor was chosen. The initial frame rate target of the thesis was 60 frames
per second. However, as a result of the unavailability of commercially available camera
sensors and open-source interface register mappings, the Raspberry Pi V2 NoIR Camera
was chosen as it was additionally compatible with the Camera Receivers of the chosen
FPGA. The specification claims a maximum frame rate of 30 frames per second at
1920x1080 resolutions over a 2-lane MIPI Interface.
==PAGE:16==
4
==PAGE:17==
CHAPTER 2
Theoretical Background
2.1
Overview
The data from the camera sensors follow the MIPI protocol. The data is handled using
the AXI Interface and the Embedded RAMs. The video streams are transmitted using
the on-board HDMI Transmitter.
2.2
Mobile Industry Processor Interface(MIPI)
High resolution videos with large frame rates are required for most image processing
applications. Most applications make use of video streams with a minimum resolution
of 1920x1080 pixels at a 30 frames per second frame rate. This high bandwidth require-
ments are impractical over conventional parallel interfaces and connectors. To alleviate
such problems, the Mobile Industry Processor Interface Camera Serial Interface(MIPI-
CSI) standards were introduced by the MIPI Alliance and has since been widely adopted.
The two-lane and four-lane MIPI specifications offer high bandwidth with compact con-
nectors.
The specification is centered around reducing the power consumption, cost
and lowering the noise produced for High-Definition Image Applications. The MIPI-CSI
Specification combines pixel and control information over up to 4 data pairs and a clock
pair. The MIPI-CSI specification is split into the following: The D-PHY Specification
for the physical layers and the CSI-protocol specification for packet transmission[5].
The unidirectional transmission of data happens in two modes: High-Speed(HS) sig-
nalling mode and Low-Power(LP) signalling mode. The High-speed mode is used for
burst transfer of pixel data in bytes while the Low-Power mode is used for control pur-
poses most importantly for syncing frames and lines of pixels. In High-speed mode the
data is transferred over low-voltage differential signals while it is single ended in low-
power mode[1].
The MIPI Signals from the camera are received and arranged by the D-PHY physical
layer on board the Trion Development Board, the details of which are explained in the
following sections.
==PAGE:18==
6
2 Theoretical Background
Figure 2.1: MIPI Transmitter and Receiver Block Diagram
2.3
Raspberry Pi v2 Camera Sensor
The Raspberry Pi v2 NoIR Camera Module is a breakout board consisting of a Sony
IMX219 8-Megapixel Image Sensor with a 2-lane MIPI Ribbon Cable connector. The
Ribbon Cable connector conists of two data lane and one clock lane differential pairs of
signals. In addition to the I2C Data and Clock signals used to set the internal registers
for the resolution, frame rate and other properties of the camera module. The data
output format of the camera module can be in RAW or YUV format. The number of
bits per pixel is also set using the I2C lines.
Figure
2.2:
Raspberry
Pi
v2
NoIR
Camera
Module[Source:

==PAGE:19==
2.4 LVDS To HDMI Transmission
7
2.4
LVDS To HDMI Transmission
The data from the image sensors after processing is transmitted over the on-board ITE
6263 LVDS to HDMI Transmission chip. The data is sent to the chip through LVDS
signals which is then transmitted over HDMI. The on-board HDMI chip is configured
automatically on connection to a monitor. The data is sent over LVDS at a rate of two
pixels per transmission clock. The pixel data is sent in RGB format with 8 bits per color.
The transmission occurs with the help of three control signals in order to synchronize
between and within frames.
The control signals being the Horizontal Sync(Hsync),
Vertical sync(Vsync) and data enable. The Vertical Sync signal is used to indicate the
end of one frame and start of a new one and the Horizontal Sync signal is used to signal
the end of a line of pixels and a start of a new one. The Hsync and Vsync signals are
’0’ during their respective SyncPulse regions and are ’1’ otherwise. The Horizontal and
Vertical Data-Enable signals indicate the Active Pixel Area. During the active pixel
area, all the control signals are active.
Figure 2.3: HDMI Transmission - Per Frame
==PAGE:20==
8
2 Theoretical Background
The total number of pixels transmitted and the rate of pixels is used to determine
the frequency of the transmission clock. For example, for a Full HD 1920x1080 pixel
transmission at a frame rate of 60 frames per second with two pixels per clock pulse, the
transmission clock is calculated using the following equations.
ClockFrequency = Rows∗Pixels∗FrameRate
NumberofPixelsperClock
ClockFrequency = 2200∗1125∗60
2
ClockFrequency = 74.25MHz
2.5
Advanced eXtensible Interface(AXI)
DDR3 Controllers
AXI is a bus protocol that enables separate read and write control interfaces with ded-
icated write and read data buses. AXI emphasises low cost, high-speed and low power
Direct Memory Access(DMA). DDR3 Controllers act as intermediaries between Host
processors and external memories.
DDR3 Memory initialisation and timing require-
ments are handled by the controller. The controller manages the memory by monitoring
the status of each of the memories and performing opening and close operations only
when required.
The AXI consists of four parts: The Shared Read/Write Signals, the write signals,
the read signals and the write response signal.
The Shared Read/Write Signals compose of signals that are common to both read
and write operations. The shared read and write signals set the common characteristics
of both read and write signals such as the address, length and burst type.
Memory can be accessed in the following burst modes: Fixed, Increment and Wrap
mode. The Fixed mode implies the address of each transfer in the burst is fixed. In
Increment mode, the address of each transfer in the transaction is automatically incre-
mented during the transaction from the shared address signal with the increment value
depending on the size of the transaction. In Wrap mode, the address increments from
the address of the transaction to the specified maximum address and will wrap back to
the start address.
Most commercially used Double Data Rate(DDR3) RAM controllers used to access
the memory employ AXI interfaces to write and read data to and from the memory.
The interface enables burst transactions capable of reading and writing large amounts
==PAGE:21==
2.5 Advanced eXtensible Interface(AXI) DDR3 Controllers
9
of data. The controllers are designed to effectively increase memory throughput while
negating effects caused by pre-charge or refreshing of memories. The entire memory
space is a continuous byte size(8-bit) memory space. In essence, meaning that a 128-bit
write operation is split into 16 individual byte sized writes and a 256-bit read consists
of 32 individual read operations.
Figure 2.4: AXI Interface[3]
The AXI Interface signals and their descriptions are as seen in the Trion 120 Datasheet
is as in the following tables, alternatively the signal definitions can also be found in the
AXI Specification. I and O are used to represent if a signal is an input to or an output
from the FPGA. _x may take the values 0 or 1 to indicate the different length interfaces.

Most commerical cameras use a Bayer filter over the sensor. The Bayer filter consists
of Red, Green and Blue(primary colors) monochrome filters over each charge coupled
device pixel sensor thus resulting in an unprocessed RAW image. This RAW image
when transmitted would have monochrome pixels with twice the amount of green color
as red and blue. Thus reducing the bandwidth and data rate transferred to the processor,
additionally leading to more compact, lower power and noise during transmission. The
RAW format essentially acts as a compression of pixel transmission data. Some RAW
formats for readout are determined by the number of bits per pixel such as 6-bit(RAW6),
8-bit(RAW8) and 10-bit(RAW10).
2.7
Demosaicing Bayer Image Output
As seen in the previous section, the missing colors of each pixel is to be calculated from
the neighbouring pixels of the required color. For this method, the widely popular and
simple Bi-Linear interpolation algorithm is employed.

==PAGE:25==
2.7 Demosaicing Bayer Image Output
13
For explaining the algorithm, the R(2,2) is considered. The missing colors for this
pixel are Blue and Green in a 3x3 matrix with the Red pixel at the centre. In order
to determine these colors, the surrounding Green and Blue Pixels are used in the com-
putation. It can be seen that the distances between these green and blue pixels are
equidistant from R(2,2) hence a symmetric bilinear interpolation algorithm is used[2][4].
This window maybe represented in the following table.
Table 2.7: Demosaicing Algorithm centered around a Red Pixel(x,y)
B(-1,-1)
Gb(0,-1)
B(1,-1)
Gr(-1,0)
R(0,0)
Gr(1,0)
B(-1,1)
Gb(0,1)
B(1,1)
Since symmetric bilinear interpolation is used to calculate the missing values, the
process of calculation becomes an average of the missing colors[4]. The value of the
green color at R(0,0) is calculated as follows:
G(0,0) = 1
4 * Σ (Gx,y), with (x,y) = { (-1,0),(0,-1),(0,1),(1,0) }
(2.1)
The value of the blue color at R(0,0) is calculated as follows:
B(0,0) = 1
4 * Σ (Bx,y), with (x,y) = { (-1,-1),(-1,1),(1,1),(1,-1) }
(2.2)
The value of red is the same as that of the pixel. These equations are used to design
the Raw-to-RGB converter. While considerations in the equation are altered during
computation of missing colors in the corner pixels. The new set of equations may be
understood by applying 0 values to the missing pixels. Similar equations are used for
missing colors of Green and Blue pixels.

==PAGE:27==
CHAPTER 3
Detailed Problem Outline
3.1
Outline
The principal problem that the project wishes to address is to use an FPGA to com-
bine any number of MIPI-input streams to produce a larger panoramic image with the
individual video streams tiled and then streamed over HDMI. The predominant require-
ment is to manage the increasing data rate as the pixel data pass through the FPGA
to be transmitted over HDMI. Since, most cameras output data in the RAW format, an
important design consideration would be to Demosaic the incoming Bayer Pixel image
to ensure transmission over HDMI. The incoming image data rate is very high, hence,
usage large register storage elements poses to be trivial. A large amount of processing
is centered around data storage and retrieval from the DDR3 Memory.
As mentioned earlier, the global shortage of electronic chips in the months around
September, 2021 played a crucial role in the selection of the FPGA. The wide unavail-
ability of ready-to-ship FPGAs capable of handling multiple MIPI inputs, lead to the
choice of the Trion T120F576 FPGA. Although the Trion series has many FPGA models
capable of handling multiple inputs, the one with highest specification is chosen so as
to not run into any resource constraints later into the project.
The lack of open source documentation for the 4-lane MIPI Raspberry Pi v1 cam-
eras(the initial choice) played a crucial role that led to its rejection. In addition to this,
the Trion T120F576 has connectors that are compatible with only 2-lane Raspberry Pi
v2 Cameras. Hence, the Raspberry Pi v2 cameras were the ideal choice for the project.
==PAGE:28==
16
3 Detailed Problem Outline
3.2
Ideal Boundary Conditions
This section intends to describe the overall boundary conditions of the project stemming
from the project outline. The ideal boundary conditions are as follows:
• Resolution of input image is greater than or equal to 1920x1080 pixels.
• Frame rate of the input video stream is 60 frames per second.
• Eﬀiciently convert the incoming RAW Video stream to RGB888(8 bits per color).
• Resolution of HDMI output is a sum of the incoming video streams.
• Frame rate of tiled output video stream is 60 frames per second.
The above mentioned boundary conditions are independent of the hardware and detail
the ideal objectives. The architecture of the design was made to achieve these objectives.
3.3
Boundary Conditions based on Selected
Hardware
While the ideal objectives provide an idea of the overall aims of the project. Some of the
objectives while they can be achieved with the architecture design, the hardware chosen
imposes a set of constraints on the project implementation. These constraints from the
hardware are a result of the chosen FPGA Development Kit and the camera sensor. The
boundary conditions arising as a result of the chosen hardware are as follows:
• The chosen Trion T120F576 board’s MIPI-input lines supports a maximum data
rate of 1.5Gbps. However, the on board provided connectors are designed for 2-
lane MIPI signals. This imposes a constraint on the maximum resolution that may
be used. The resolution of 1920x1080 pixels is however still achievable from the
camera.
• The chosen camera module being 2-lane MIPI signals has a maximum output
frame rate.
As seen in the specification sheet, the maximum output from the
camera module is a Full HD(1920x1080 pixels) at a 30 frame per second frame
rate.
• The FPGA interacts with the DDR3 Memory using the AXI interface. Each AXI
Interface has two sub-modules of different lengths(128 bit and 256 bit). Both sub-
modules are capable of read and write operations. So as to reduce read and write
conflicts each sub-module is used exclusively for read and write operations. Since
the incoming pixels are buffered to be stored in the DDR3 Memory, the 128-bit
==PAGE:29==
3.4 Programmer Software Constraints
17
sub-module is used to write data to the memroy and to enable higher throughput
during processing the 256-bit sub-module is used for reading from the memory.
• The Trion T120F576 Development kit has an on-board LVDS to HDMI Transmit-
ter. The transmitter has a maximum output specification of ’1920x1080’ pixels
at 60 frames per second. The output format of the pixels is the RGB888 format
with 24 bits per pixel and 8 per color. The boundary conditions imposed by this
piece of hardware plays a big role in the architecture of the design. The maximum
output being ’1920x1080’ implies that the incoming video streams can not be tiled
to produce a larger panoramic image.
Hence, the incoming image although is
processed fully, during transmission, only half the image is transmitted for each
video input. This can be essentially understood from the output perspective in
the following way: For two input ’1920x1080’ video streams, 960 pixels from each
stream is selected and tiled and transmitted. For three input ’1920x1080’ video
streams, 640 pixels from each camera is selected and tiled to produce a panoramic
image. In the case where a higher resolution HDMI transmitter is available, the
output module may be used to transmitted the image without any cropping.
3.4
Programmer Software Constraints
The IDE Software poses an important constraint based on which a design constraints
were placed. The IDE Software limits the maximum bit vector length of about 12,000
bits thereby limiting the Logic RAM that can be synthesised to handle the 792-bit RGB
logic vectors from the RGB Converter. Hence, to overcome this problem a memory
controller is designed to read the 792-bit RGB data and extrapolate 24-bit pixel values
which are then stored into 24-bit odd and even pixel RAMs with 1024 locations. These
are then read by the HDMI transmitter.
Initially, the entire project was designed in VHDL. It was chosen due to its easy
readability and it being a language that has been previously used in multiple design
projects. However, during synthesis a Verilator program that inevitably converts the
entire program into a Verilog HDL final file over span of four hours. The pragmatic
choice was to design the individual modules using VHDL and the top module in Verilog
HDL, this reduced the complete synthesis time to five minutes.
As a result of the Synthesis program removing some inputs as a result of them
not being directly controlled in the module, some important inputs are used to trigger
unconnected outputs from the FGPA. This is done to ensure their synthesis.

18
==PAGE:31==

following diagram[4.1].
Figure 4.1: Black Box of the FPGA

Architecture Introduction
To simplify the explanation for the architecture of the accelerator it is explained from
the perspective of one camera. Towards the end of the section, the design is explained
for multiple cameras.
==PAGE:32==
20
4 Architecture And Design Overview
Figure 4.2: Architecture from the perspective of one camera
==PAGE:33==
4.3 Architecture Description
21
4.3
Architecture Description
4.3.1
MIPI Receiver
In addition to the designed components, the instantiation and configuration of the in-
ternal registers of the camera over I2C are performed using the modules provided by
Eﬀinix in their example programs for the Raspberry Pi v2 Camera.
The MIPI Receiver’s primary function is to accept incoming MIPI data at 10 bits
per pixel and produce a buffered 128-bit output of 16 pixels each of 8 bits. The two
least significant bits(LSB) are discarded to reduce the data rate of storage and retrieval
from the memory and through the processor. The output pixel format has to be in
RGB format with 24 bits per pixel for transmission over HDMI hence disregarding of
the LSBs has no significant impact on the picture quality. Since the RGB Converter was
designed for an RGGB Format but the incoming pixels are of GRBG format the pixels
are interchanged during packing so as to produce the RGGB format. The description of
the packer is a FIFO that has an enable signal after 4 MIPI inputs have been buffered.
The 64-bit Input Bus from the on board MIPI D-PHY receiver network is as below.
Figure 4.3: MIPI Data from the On-board D-PHY network
The rippling of the MIPI Input through the buffer is as follows:
==PAGE:34==

Figure 4.4: MIPI Receiver Module Overview
4.3.1.1
Interface Programming of the FPGA in the IDE
The MIPI Interface is instantiated in the IDE of Eﬀinix Trion. The same signal names
are to be used in the top module of the project. The instantiated signals are as follows:
==PAGE:35==
4.3 Architecture Description
23
Figure 4.5: MIPI Interface Instantiation
4.3.1.2
Interface Signal Description
The Horizontal Sync signal is an active-high signal used to identify a valid pixels in a
line and to sync between lines of an image. The Vertical Sync signal is used to identify
valid lines of pixels. There may be scenarios where either of the signals are active. The
Horizontal Sync signal being active with the Vertical Sync signal inactive implies that
it is a valid pixel in an invalid line and hence is not to be considered. If the Vertical
Sync is active and the Horizontal Sync is inactive it implies that it is invalid pixels in
a valid line and is to be not considered. Both signals being active indicates valid pixels
and valid lines. The Valid signal acts as a data enable signal during the data transfer
over the MIPI Interface. The ’CNT’ signal is used to indicate the number of valid pixels
in the MIPI Data bus. The ’TYPE’ signal is used to determine the video type such as
RAW10, RAW8 using hexadecimal codes.
==PAGE:36==
24
4 Architecture And Design Overview
The output signals ’VC_ENA’ and ’LANES’ output signals are used to set the
Virtual channel over which the MIPI Data is transferred and the LANES signal is used
to set the number of lanes over which MIPI Data is transferred.
4.4
AXI Write Inteface
4.4.1
Functional Description
Out of the two sub-modules of the AXI Inteface, the 128-bit module is used for write
transactions. The AMBA AXI Protocol is followed for the transfer. The state machine
of the Write Interface is as seen in the below figure. The enable signal from the MIPI
Receiver once it has buffered 4 MIPI Databus inputs being active triggers the write
statemachine. At the end of every write transction the 32-bit address bus register of the
AXI Interface is incremented by 16 as the data is stored byte-wise. The internal address
counter, horizontal and vertical pixel counters reset as the horizontal and vertical pixel
counter reach 1919 and 1079 respectively(as the counter begins from 0).

4.5
AXI Read Interface
4.5.1
Functional Description
The larger data-width sub-module of the AXI Interface, the 256-bit module is used for
the read transactions. The State machine of the read interface is designed to use the
start signal from the HDMI Transmitter to begin a series of read transactions that read
effectively one entire pixel row(in addition to two other rows) in the memory and before
coming to a stop. This occurs till all the pixel rows are read. Each read operation cycle
consists of three individual pixel row reads. The read pixels are then transmitted to the
RGB Converter with the horizontal and vertical pixel counter values. The state machine
of the Read interface is as follows:
Figure 4.9: Read Interface State Machine
The individual signals of the read interface can be seen in Table 2.2.

4.6
Raw to RGB Converter
4.6.1
Functional Description
The Raw signals are converted into RGB Signals using the algorithms presented in Sec-
tion 2.7. The various corner cases are identified for the RGB Converter and are dealt
with individually. The converter uses the horizontal and vertical counter to determine
what cluster of pixels has been read. A total of 60 x 32-pixel reads and interpolations
are performed per pixel row.
The processing in the converter is dependant on the AXI Reads. The 256-bit during
the read process reads a total of 32 pixels per row per read operation and a total of
three rows per transaction. Since every pixel needs the value of the neighbouring pixel
for interpolation during start and the middle of the row(except the end interpolation),
the end partial calculation is buffered and computed in the next iteration. During the
start of the row, the first 31 pixels out of the 32 pixel values read are computed and
stored. The final pixel value of the previous iteration is computed in the next iteration
with the final pixel of that row being buffered for the next iteration. At the end of
each pixel row a total of 33 pixels are computed as a result of the previous pixel and
end pixel being computed with no buffering. To increase the throughput of the system,
during the processing of the first row, the second row is computed as well and during
the processing of the last row, the previous row is computed as well. The computation
lengths are calculated as follows:
1. Start of row processing: All pixels except the last pixel are computed and stored
==PAGE:41==
4.6 Raw to RGB Converter
29
in the memory. Hence, the total number of pixels processed is 31. Hence, the total
length of the output signal is 24 bit per pixel * 31 pixels = 744 bits.
2. Middle of row processing: In addition to the last pixel of the previous computation,
the remaining pixels except the last pixel is computed and stored in the memory.
The number of pixels being processed is 32. Hence, the total length of the output
signal is 24 bit per pixel * 32 pixels = 768 bits.
3. End of row processing: In the final iteration of a row, the last pixel of the previous
iteration in addition to the remaining 32 pixels is processed. The total number of
pixels processed here is 33 pixels. Hence, the total length of the output bus is 24
bit per pixel * 33 pixels = 792 bits.
The output signal is of length 792-bit to fit the maximum throughput case. The output
signal is zero-padded to fill the missing bits.
The symmetric bilinear-interpolation operations are computed by right
shifts and additions.
==PAGE:42==
30
4 Architecture And Design Overview
4.6.2

4.6.2.1
Top left Corner
As can be seen in the following figure, the first 31 pixels are computed with the 32nd pixel
being partially computed and buffered for the next operation. In this case, both Red
and Blue rows of pixels are computed simultaneously. The border pixels are calculated
by using only the available values this reducing the amount of right shift during the
operation.
Figure 4.11: Top Left Corner

4.7
RGB RAM Controller
4.7.1
Functional Description
The 792-bit data from the RGB Converter is stored in a 792-bit word RAM with a total
of 64 locations. There are two RAMs used for this purpose, for each odd and even row.
The state-machine is started by the write-enable signal of the RGB Converter. The
controller is designed to handle the different output lengths of the RGB Converter. For
each read, the same steps are followed. The steps are a read-state for reading from the
RAM and a read of individual 24-bit pixels from the 792-bit word before moving on to
the next state.
The first data read from the 792-bit RAM at address zero, is split into 31 pixels which
are stored into two RAMs that store the odd and even pixels of a row. The second data
read up till the last data read at addresses between 1 and 58 are read and split into 32
pixels which are then stored into the even and odd pixel RAMs. For the final read at
location 59, the data is split into 33 pixels and stored in the respective odd and even
RAMs.
This split into two RAMs is done since the HDMI Transmitter transmits two pixels
at a time, hence splitting into odd and even pixel RAMs enables parallel access of data
and transmission of the pixel values over the HDMI Interface.
4.8
HDMI Odd and Even Pixel Dual-Port-RAM
The dual port RAMs used for transmitting the pixel values operate on two clocks for
the read and write operations. The write operations are done on the ’AXI interface’
clock and the HDMI Transmission interface clock. The Dual Port RAM Modules used
in the project were the Dual Port RAM Modules provided by Eﬀinix, available on their
==PAGE:47==
4.9 HDMI Tranmission Controller
35
website(https://www.efinixinc.com/support/kb/faq.php?id=38).
The word-length of the RAM is 24-bits. With a total length of 960. These RAMs
together constitute the 1080 pixels of the final image. The read operation is handled by
the HDMI Interface while the write operation is handled by the RGB Ram Controller.
4.9
HDMI Tranmission Controller
The HDMI Transmission Controller follows the specifications mentioned in Section 2.4.
The HDMI Transmission Controller does not use a state machine to control the output
signals. Rather, two counters concerning the horizontal and vertical pixel values are used
to determine the output signals. Compared to a state machine, a functional description
would serve as a better tool to explain the Controller.
4.9.1
Functional Description
Figure 4.23: HDMI Frame Overview
The horizontal counter is increased by 1 every clock cycle. As the transmission is done
at a rate of two pixels per clock cycle, the values of pixel values to control the output
signals is half as that mentioned in Section 2.4. The vertical counter is incremented
by 1 when the horizontal counter reaches the end pixel ,i.e, 960. The data enable signal
is used to determine the valid pixels within the frame, thus it is active in the range
==PAGE:48==
36
4 Architecture And Design Overview
{96,1056} for the horizontal counter and {30,1110} for the vertical counter.
As the counters approach the active pixel region, the read operation is started specifi-
cally at pixel count ’94’, this is done to provide adequate time for the access of the RAM
and the register clocking the read data. The read enable and read address are activated
and incremented during the active area. The odd and even pixel RAMs are accessed
according to the LSB of the vertical counter being ’0’ or ’1’, indicating even or odd
respectively. The address registers are then reset at the end of each pixel row. There
are two RAMs to store the even and odd pixels of each row. Additionally, there are
two sets of such RAMs as the odd and even row of pixels are computed simultaneously
but stored separately. The reason for this approach, is that by attaching the HDMI
Transmission to the multiple memories of each camera, different video streams maybe
interlaced.
The Horizontal sync signal are inactive at the end of a line of pixels. It is then active
after the H-Sync-Pulse interval. The Vertical sync signal is inactive at the end of a frame
and is then active after the Vertical Sync Pulse interval. The pixel data is transmitted
in RGB888 format with a total of 24 bits per pixel at 8 bits per color. The data and the
control signals are interlaced in the output signals. There are a total of 8 output signals
each of 7 bit length. The read-interface start operations are triggered when it is in the
previous row of the active pixels and during which it is enabled at the 400th pixel and
deactivated at the 500th pixel transmission. The data are transmitted as follows:
• LVDS-1A : { Pixel-Even-Red[0 to 5] , Pixel-Even-Green[0] }
• LVDS-1B : {Pixel-Even-Green[1 to 5] , Pixel-Even-Blue[0 to 1] }
• LVDS-1C : { Pixel-Even-Blue[2 to 5] , Horizontal-Sync, Vertical-Sync, Data-Enable }
• LVDS-1D : { Pixel-Even-Red[6 to 7] , Pixel-Even-Green[6 to 7] , Pixel-Even-Red[6 to 7],
’0’}
• LVDS-2A : { Pixel-Odd-Red[0 to 5] , Pixel-Odd-Green[0] }
• LVDS-2B : { Pixel-Odd-Green[1 to 5] , Pixel-Odd-Blue[0 to 1] }
• LVDS-2C : { Pixel-Odd-Blue[2 to 5] , Horizontal-Sync, Vertical-Sync, Data-Enable }
• LVDS-2D : { Pixel-Odd-Red[6 to 7] , Pixel-Odd-Green[6 to 7] , Pixel-Odd-Red[6 to 7],
’0’}
4.9.2
Final Idea for an HDMI Transmitter
In order to combine multiple video streams, the design up till the HDMI Transmitter
is replicated for each camera, thus ending at the 24-bit RAMs. As the counters of the
==PAGE:49==
4.9 HDMI Tranmission Controller
37
HDMI Transmitter enter the active pixel area, the multiple images are combined by
accessing the different memories for each camera. For example, considering the case
of two cameras, as the camera has transmitted 960(x2 pixels) of one camera and the
next set of 24-bit memories of the other camera are accessed and transmitted. Thus,
producing no border pixel line or any distortions, resulting in a long tiled panoramic
image.
==PAGE:50==
38
==PAGE:51==
CHAPTER 5
Results
5.1
Synthesis Results
On the end of the synthesis process, the following resource usage is obtained.
Figure 5.1: Resource Usage - Synthesis Summary
The inter-clock relationships can be seen as follows. The lower axi_clk frequency
is as a result of the ”start-read” signal from the HDMI Transmission controller(slower
clock) into the read-interface(faster clock) but this does not cause errors in the operation
of the design, as the start-read signal remains active for 100 clock cycles.
==PAGE:52==
40
5 Results
Figure 5.2: Resource Usage - Synthesis Summary
==PAGE:53==
5.2 Power Consumption
41
5.2
Power Consumption
The provided Excel document to calculate an estimate of the power is used. A very high
activity factor of 60% is used to calculate the highest possible power consumption. While
it is known that the FPGA would never attain that level of activity, it is interesting to
note that even at that level, the FPGA consumes less than a Watt of power to handle
and transmit data from one camera.
Figure 5.3: Power Consumption Estimation
==PAGE:54==
42
5 Results
5.3
HDMI Output
The design was tested for one camera. Instead of the output of the second camera, a
red color was used to indicate the place of the output.
Figure 5.4: Output as seen on a Monitor
5.4
Future Work
The future work would revolve around further optimization of the pipeline. At present,
however, there are some screen tearing issues between frames. Work would need to be
done to explore smoother interactions between the different stages of the pipeline. The
current RGB pixel values have a higher blue hue, hence a better or possibly more complex
color correction algorithm such as a quadratic interpolation algorithm or possibly an
RGB Gain module could be implemented to correct the ratio of different colors of the
image. Another optimization would be to use a faster clock to retrieve and access the
DDR3 memories and use the same clock throughout the pipeline, however, there would
need to be cross-domain synchronization of the operations.
5.5
Conclusion
The main idea of the project is to utilize the high-speed frequencies and large parallelism
from the logic elements to process and combine multiple high-speed video streams. The
objective is to receive, process and store pixel data into a memory which can then be
read in any order using the read enable and read address signals to combine and output
==PAGE:55==
5.5 Conclusion
43
the stored video data.
The design was made such that it can be replicated based on the number of cameras.
The final HDMI Transmitter module would have to be modified to access the memories
of the newly added cameras based on the output pixel coordinates. In case, the output
interface is changed to, for example, Ethernet then the same memories would then have
to be accessed by the Ethernet output controller. Hence, the architecture design stands
independent of the output method or interface. It is rather dependant on the number of
MIPI Inputs and the number of DDR3 AXI Memories(One per camera). This concept
has been demonstrated successfully.

25 ------------------------------------------------------------------------
26 --
LOGIC
27 --
CASES OF STATEMACHINE:
28 --
A) FIRST ROW + PARTIAL PRODUCT OF SECOND ROW
29 --
1) FIRST TWO ROWS + FIRST COL
30 --
2) FIRST TWO ROWS + MIDDLE COL (requires 1 col before and 1
col after)
31 --
3) FIRST TWO ROWS
+ LAST COL => After this save row 2 and
read row 3 and 4
32 --
B)PARTIAL PRODUCT OF SECOND ROW + ROW 3
33 ------------------------------------------------------------------------
34 ------------------------------------------------------------------------
35 --
A) FIRST ROW: 1) Pixel 00(R)- R = R, G= Gr_10 + Gr1/2 ; B = B
36 --
2) Pixel 0,1(G) : G = G, R00 + R02/2 ; B = B11
37 --
B) SECOND - SECOND -LAST ROW: COMMON CASE
38 --
1) FIRST COL: Green row -- ODD ROWS UP TILL 30th pixel
39 --
2) MIDDLE COL : Green row
40 --
3) LAST COL : GREEN ROW
41 --
i) FIRST COL: RED row -- EVEN ROWS UP TILL 31st pixel
42 --
ii) MIDDLE COL : RED ROW
43 --
iii) LAST COL : RED ROW
44 -- COMMON CASE: i) R ROw ( EVEN ROW): -
45
46
47 -- Coordinate system 0,0 - Upper left corner: Pix_x-value_y -value
48 -- R(0,0) - 7 DOWNTO 0 ; Gr(1,0) - 15 DOWNTO 8 ; R(2,0) - 23 DOWNTO 16
49 -- Gb(0,1) - 7 DOWNTO 0 ; B(1,1) - 15 DOWNTO 8; Gb(2,1) - 23DOWNTO 16
50 --
|----------------------|---------------------|--------------------
51 --
|--------------------|--------------------|--------------------
52 --
|--------------------|--------------------|--------------------
53 --
|--------------------|--------------------|--------------------
54 --
R(0,1078)
55 --
Gb(0,1079)
